style="top:-2.357em;margin-left:0em;margin-right:0.07142857142857144em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height:2.5em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size3 size1 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.143em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.286108em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.0037em;">α</span><span class="mord">/2</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span></p>
</li>
</ol></div><div class="textBlock"><p>Here we list the <strong>most important tests that produce F-scores: each of them is right-tailed</strong>.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>ANOVA</strong>: tests the equality of <strong>means in three or more groups</strong> that come from normally distributed populations with equal variances. There are <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>k</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>n</mi><mo>−</mo><mi>k</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(k - 1, n - k)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.8388800000000001em;vertical-align:-0.19444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.16666666666666666em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> degrees of freedom, where <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">k</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.69444em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span></span></span></span></span> is the number of groups, and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">n</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.43056em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span></span></span></span></span> is the total sample size (across every group).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Overall significance in regression analysis</strong>. The test statistic has <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>k</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>n</mi><mo>−</mo><mi>k</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(k - 1, n - k)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.8388800000000001em;vertical-align:-0.19444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.16666666666666666em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> degrees of freedom, where <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">n</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.43056em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span></span></span></span></span> is the sample size, and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">k</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.69444em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span></span></span></span></span> is the number of variables (including the intercept).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Compare two nested regression models</strong>. The test statistic follows the F-distribution with <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>n</mi><mo>−</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(k_2 - k_1, n - k_2)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.30110799999999993em;"><span style="top:-2.5500000000000003em;margin-left:-0.03148em;margin-right:0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height:2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.8888799999999999em;vertical-align:-0.19444em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.30110799999999993em;"><span style="top:-2.5500000000000003em;margin-left:-0.03148em;margin-right:0.05em;"><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.30110799999999993em;"><span mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> degrees of freedom, where <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">k_1</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.84444em;vertical-align:-0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.30110799999999993em;"><span xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">k_2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.84444em;vertical-align:-0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right:0.03148em;">k</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height:0.30110799999999993em;"><span mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">n</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.43056em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span></span></span></span></span> is the sample size.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>The equality of variances in two normally distributed populations</strong>. There are <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>n</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>m</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(n - 1, m - 1)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.8388800000000001em;vertical-align:-0.19444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.16666666666666666em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">m</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right:0.2222222222222222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:1em;vertical-align:-0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> degrees of freedom, where <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">n</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.43056em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">n</span></span></span></span></span> and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">m</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height:0.43056em;vertical-align:0em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">m</span></span></span></span></span> are the respective sample sizes.</p>
</li>
</ul></div></div></div></section><section data-testid="articleSection-11" data-cwv-id="calculator-article-section" id="behind-the-scenes-of-the-critical-value-calculator" class="css-qtz96j e1p70fvu0"><h2 data-testid="articleSectionHeader-11" class="css-1xbj7gb">Behind the scenes of the critical value calculator</h2><div data-testid="articleSectionText" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>I'm Anna, the mastermind behind the critical value calculator and a <strong>PhD in mathematics from Jagiellonian University</strong>.</p>
<p>The idea for creating the tool originated from my experiences in teaching and research. Recognizing the need for a <strong>tool that simplifies the critical value determination process</strong> across various statistical distributions, I built a user-friendly calculator accessible to both students and professionals. After publishing the tool, I soon found myself using the calculator in my research and as a teaching aid.</p>
<p>Trust in this calculator is paramount to me. Each tool undergoes a <strong>rigorous review process</strong>, featuring peer-reviewed insights from experts and meticulous proofreading by native speakers. This commitment to accuracy and reliability ensures that users can be confident in our content. Please check our <a href="/editorial-policies" class="js-link">Editorial Policies page</a> for more details on our standards.</p></div></div></div></section><section data-testid="articleSection-12" data-cwv-id="calculator-article-section" id="faqs" class="css-qtz96j e1p70fvu0"><h2 data-testid="articleSectionHeader-12" class="css-1xbj7gb">FAQs</h2><div data-testid="articleSectionText" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><style data-emotion="css 1ugw61y">.css-1ugw61y{padding:0px;margin:24px 0px 16px;color:#212427;text-align:left;font-family:var(--calculator-ui-font-family);font-size:18.6px;font-style:normal;font-weight:600;line-height:28px;}</style><h3 data-testid="What is a Z critical value?" class="css-1ugw61y">What is a Z critical value?</h3><div data-testid="What is a Z critical value?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>A Z critical value is the value that defines the <strong>critical region in hypothesis testing</strong> when the test statistic follows the <strong>standard normal distribution</strong>. If the value of the test statistic falls into the critical region, you should reject the null hypothesis and accept the alternative hypothesis.</p></div></div></div><h3 data-testid="How do I calculate Z critical value?" class="css-1ugw61y">How do I calculate Z critical value?</h3><div data-testid="How do I calculate Z critical value?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>To find a Z critical value for a given confidence level <code>α</code>:</p>
<ol>
<li>Check if you perform a <strong>one- or two-tailed test</strong>.</li>
<li>For a one-tailed test:
<ul>
<li><strong>Left</strong>-tailed: critical value is the <strong><code>α</code>-th</strong> quantile of the standard normal distribution N(0,1).</li>
<li><strong>Right</strong>-tailed: critical value is the <strong><code>(1-α)</code>-th</strong> quantile.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Two-tailed test: critical value equals <strong><code>±(1-α/2)</code>-th</strong> quantile of N(0,1).</li>
<li>No <strong>quantile tables</strong>? Use CDF tables! (The quantile function is the inverse of the CDF.)</li>
<li>Verify your answer with an online critical value calculator.</li>
</ol></div></div></div><h3 data-testid="Is a t critical value the same as Z critical value?" class="css-1ugw61y">Is a t critical value the same as Z critical value?</h3><div data-testid="Is a t critical value the same as Z critical value?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>In theory, <strong>no</strong>. In practice, very often, <strong>yes</strong>. The t-Student distribution is <strong>similar</strong> to the standard normal distribution, but it is <strong>not the same</strong>. However, if the number of <strong>degrees of freedom</strong> (which is, roughly speaking, the size of your sample) is large enough (&gt;30), then the two distributions are <strong>practically indistinguishable</strong>, and so the t critical value has practically the same value as the Z critical value.</p></div></div></div><h3 data-testid="What is the Z critical value for 95% confidence?" class="css-1ugw61y">What is the Z critical value for 95% confidence?</h3><div data-testid="What is the Z critical value for 95% confidence?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>The Z critical value for a 95% confidence interval is:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>1.96</strong> for a two-tailed test;</li>
<li><strong>1.64</strong> for a right-tailed test; and</li>
<li><strong>-1.64</strong> for a left-tailed test.</li>
</ul></div></div></div><h3 data-testid="What is the significance level, and how is it used in hypothesis testing?" class="css-1ugw61y">What is the significance level, and how is it used in hypothesis testing?</h3><div data-testid="What is the significance level, and how is it used in hypothesis testing?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>The significance level is a pre-determined threshold used to decide whether to reject the null hypothesis in a statistical test. The most commonly used significance level is 0.05, which means there is a 5% probability of rejecting the null hypothesis when it is actually true.</p></div></div></div><h3 data-testid="What are the Null hypothesis and alternate hypothesis?" class="css-1ugw61y">What are the Null hypothesis and alternate hypothesis?</h3><div data-testid="What are the Null hypothesis and alternate hypothesis?-answer" class="css-h9evcv e1p70fvu1"><div class="e1yds0yu0 css-12nedol e1m4c3o81"><div class="textBlock"><p>The null hypothesis (H₀) states that there is no significant difference between the two parameters, such as growth, weight, or any other measured effect. In contrast, the alternative hypothesis (H₁) suggests that there is a significant difference between the two parameters.</p></div></div></div></div></section></div><style data-emotion="css 1awdiu7">.css-1awdiu7{margin-top:24px;padding:0;background:none;border:0;box-shadow:none;}.css-1awdiu7.css-1awdiu7 .MuiCollapse-root,.css-1awdiu7 The other approach is to calculate the p-value (for example, using the \u003ca href=\"/statistics/p-value\" class=\"js-link\"\u003ep-value calculator\u003c/a\u003e).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe critical value approach consists of checking if the value of the test statistic generated by your sample belongs to the so-called \u003cem\u003erejection region\u003c/em\u003e, or \u003cem\u003ecritical region\u003c/em\u003e, which is the \u003cstrong\u003eregion where the test statistic is highly improbable to lie\u003c/strong\u003e. A critical value is a cut-off value (or two cut-off values in the case of a two-tailed test) that constitutes the boundary of the rejection region(s). In other words, critical values divide the scale of your test statistic into the rejection region and the non-rejection region.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOnce you have found the rejection region, \u003cstrong\u003echeck if the value of the test statistic generated by your sample belongs to it\u003c/strong\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIf so, it means that you can reject the null hypothesis and accept the alternative hypothesis; and\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIf not, then there is not enough evidence to reject H\u003csub\u003e0\u003c/sub\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut how to calculate critical values?
